<div id="review_2970000884" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2970000884" />
    <a title="Jan Rice" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5864678-jan-rice"><img alt="Jan Rice" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1497584438p2/5864678.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2970000884?book_show_action=true">Nov 22, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jan Rice" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jan Rice" href="/user/show/5864678-jan-rice">Jan Rice</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780156032522" href="/book/show/31128690-a-tale-of-love-and-darkness">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5864678-jan-rice?shelf=book-club-selection">book-club-selection</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5864678-jan-rice?shelf=biography-memoir">biography-memoir</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2970000884" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14979371583524220046">What would I like to tell you about this book?<br/><br/>You might have thought it was about Israel, that that's what the title means. Well, yes, but it's more about the author's family; more memoir than history, yet solidly grounded in history. I'm in awe that anybody could learn that much about his family, to have listened and remembered and sought out the surviving family and friends of the generations that came before, then channeled them. And, oh, yeah, it's about books, too. And writing. It's about "</span>
  <span id="freeText14979371583524220046" style="display:none">What would I like to tell you about this book?<br /><br />You might have thought it was about Israel, that that's what the title means. Well, yes, but it's more about the author's family; more memoir than history, yet solidly grounded in history. I'm in awe that anybody could learn that much about his family, to have listened and remembered and sought out the surviving family and friends of the generations that came before, then channeled them. And, oh, yeah, it's about books, too. And writing. It's about "the woman question," about his mother -- oh, his mother! About the older generation, moving from a nineteenth century eastern European mentality, where they had lived up to the expectations of bourgeois society while striving to live down the expectations of Christians about Jews, until they had internalized impossibly demanding standards (and sometimes lived up to them!), then, as the vise tightened, jumping from there to a rough-and-ready frontier society of shortages and new demands, with yet another, more foreign, set of ideals and standards. And about books, language, and love (and fear), learning and ignorance, endurance and more fear, and curiosity and honesty and sometimes hard-won understanding, which he distills into this book.<br /><br />About how, within his father's and grandparents' lifetimes, they saw the graffiti reading "Yids, get out of here, go to Palestine" change to "Yids, <i>get out</i> of Palestine.<br /><br />About how in Europe before World War II, the Jews were the <i>only</i> Europeans; everybody else was a Pole or a German or Russian -- some kind of nationalist. <br /><br />Here I have one of my bits of unfinished business: friends bragging on accomplished Jews during the interwar period who identified as Europeans, not as Jews. Would those friends ever praise people of color for not identifying as such, or celebrate Muslims or native peoples for jettisoning their identity? Think about it.<br /><br />There was his uncle who died standing up for that European ideal, along with wife and not-quite-three-year-old son (Amos' little cousin). The uncle who was so outstanding in his field of comparative literature, knew so many languages, had so much expertise, that he was given a university post <i>despite</i> being a Jew. And so he stayed in behind in Vilna, refusing to give in to hate, until the end.<br /><br />There's a two-and-a-half page section at the end of Chapter 51 that's worth all of Ari Shavit's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15798334.My_Promised_Land_The_Triumph_and_Tragedy_of_Israel" title="My Promised Land The Triumph and Tragedy of Israel by Ari Shavit" rel="nofollow noopener">My Promised Land: The Triumph and Tragedy of Israel</a>, or at least worth a thousand times the excerpt <i>The New Yorker</i> published. Amos is by then a teen-aged kibbutznik who refers to the Palestinians (not yet known by that name) as "murderers," whereupon his older mentor with whom he's on night-watch duty takes him for a metaphorical walk in the other's shoes. Amos then asks if the Jews have now stolen someone else' land, while also pointing out that most of the Jews' own ancestors were themselves driven out by force two millennia ago. In brief, the mentor tells him that Jews, like everyone else, deserve a homeland.<br /><br /><blockquote>It's really very simple. Where is the Jewish people's land if not here? Under the sea? On the moon?</blockquote><br /><br />And then Amos asks him about having taken this land from them, to which he replies:<br /><br /><blockquote>...maybe you happen to have forgotten that in '48 they had a go at killing all of us? Then, in '48, there was a terrible war, and they themselves made it a simple question of either them or us, and we won and took it from them. It's nothing to boast about! But if they'd beaten us in '48, there would have been even less to boast about: they wouldn't have left a single Jew alive. And it's true there isn't a single Jew living in the whole of their sector today. ...<br /><br />(and if they turn up here shooting at us tonight while we're on watch, then what?) We'll just have to lie down in the mud and shoot. And we'll try our damnedest to shoot better and faster than them. But we won't shoot at them because they're a nation of murderers, but for the simple reason that we also have a right to live and for the simple reason that we also have a right to a land of our own. Not just them. ....</blockquote><br /><br />I want to close with an entirely different topic, "the woman question," which had two parts and which impacted everybody, not only the women. <br /><br />First, among the European bourgeoisie from which Amos Oz' people hailed, women were considered to have a right to get an education and to use it, too -- up until the first baby came along. That was it. Once she had raised her children, her role was in nurturing the grandchildren. The answer to when it was time to get back to becoming yourself: never. <br /><br />Second is the question of how sex was handled pre-birth control and in a traditional society, if you were a girl from a good home, and especially if you were from a minority population trying to be doubly good. The answer is that the subject was verboten. Sex education, nil; fear of flying, max. His aunt, a great source of information, could refer to the topic only as "between him and her," and the image she used over and over was of walking barefoot in a dark cellar full of scorpions. And of being on guard every waking moment and even in sleep.<br /><br />We are more familiar with the Madonna-whore dichotomy, but this went beyond, with males, too, indoctrinated, therefore not so likely in this matter to be a source of help to their wives.<br /><br />Such a bunch of screwed-up women in these pages! They took the brunt.<br /><br />Tension and stress abounded, with everybody driven to hang on -- but you can't hold your breath indefinitely -- and to adapt, learn, create. <br /><br />Through their often inadvertent sacrifices, they laid the foundation.<br /><br />And I've only just touched on what fills this book of over 500 pages.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14979371583524220046" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_716722114" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/716722114" />
    <a title="Lisa (Harmonybites)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2596042-lisa-harmonybites"><img alt="Lisa (Harmonybites)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1337396485p2/2596042.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/716722114?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa (Harmonybites)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa (Harmonybites)" href="/user/show/2596042-lisa-harmonybites">Lisa (Harmonybites)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fans of Israeli Literature?</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2596042-lisa-harmonybites?shelf=2013-around-the-world">2013-around-the-world</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2596042-lisa-harmonybites?shelf=biography">biography</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2596042-lisa-harmonybites?shelf=religion">religion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2596042-lisa-harmonybites?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2596042-lisa-harmonybites?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer716722114" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5281753077728444396">Amos Oz is considered one of <i>the</i> leading lights of Israeli literature and there's buzz he's a candidate for a Nobel Prize. This particular book, his memoir, "was nominated one of the ten most important books since the creation of the State of Israel." And at times I truly can understand why. The man can turn a phrase and some of his insights are as striking as his prose: <br/><br/><i>That wordatlarge was far away, attractive, marvelous, but to us it was dangerous and threatening. It didn't like the Jews beca</i></span>
  <span id="freeText5281753077728444396" style="display:none">Amos Oz is considered one of <i>the</i> leading lights of Israeli literature and there's buzz he's a candidate for a Nobel Prize. This particular book, his memoir, "was nominated one of the ten most important books since the creation of the State of Israel." And at times I truly can understand why. The man can turn a phrase and some of his insights are as striking as his prose: <br /><br /><i>That wordatlarge was far away, attractive, marvelous, but to us it was dangerous and threatening. It didn't like the Jews because they were clever, quick-witted, successful, but also because they were noisy and pushy. It didn't like what we were doing here in the Land of Israel either, because it begrudged us even this meager strip of marshland, boulders, and desert. Out there, in the world, all the walls were covered with graffiti: "Yids, go back to Palestine," so we came back to Palestine, and now the worldatlarge shouts at us: "Yids, get out of Palestine."</i><br /><br />AND<br /><br /><i>My father had a sensual relationship with his books. He loved feeling them, stroking them, sniffing them... books then really were sexier than books today: they were good to sniff and stroke and fondle. There were books with gold writing on fragrant, slightly rough leather bindings, that gave you gooseflesh when you touched them, as though you were groping something private and inaccessible, something that seemed to tremble at your touch. And there were other books that were bound in cloth-covered cardboard, stuck with a glue that had a wonderful smell. Every book had its own private, provocative scent. Sometimes the cloth came away from the cardboard, like a saucy skirt, and it was hard to resist the temptation to peep into the dark space between body and clothing and sniff those dizzying smells.</i><br /><br />So, why rate the book so low? The best I can describe my experience in reading this book is that it was like being sat tightly wedged on a sofa between two proud parents who are determined to show you the family albums--stacks of them--and natter on and on about each photograph. Maybe I'd have felt differently were this not my first work by Amos and I were already a fan of his. Or if I better knew and cared about Israeli intelligentsia and literati. The political and military figures of Israel's short history I generally know and find interesting. It's certainly an eventful, fascinating history--but for me Amos made this story of him and his family before, during, and after the birth of Israel dull as dirt. The memoir was repetitive and rambling and overlong and I gave it over 200 pages before deciding I didn't want to suffer through 400 more.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5281753077728444396" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_105271406" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/105271406" />
    <a title="Robert Cortvriendt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3815500-robert-cortvriendt"><img alt="Robert Cortvriendt" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/105271406?book_show_action=true">Jun 01, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robert Cortvriendt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robert Cortvriendt" href="/user/show/3815500-robert-cortvriendt">Robert Cortvriendt</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer105271406" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1843466473387895508">I selected this book after reading a news story about a Palestinian lawyer personally financing its translation into Arabic for his friends and family to read, his name is Elias Khoury. He was moved by a personal account through the eyes of a young boy about the birth of Israel, its families and real world strife; he hoped for better understanding between the warring sides. I thought this must be some book. It is.<br/>I've always been cloudy on the subject of the Israeli conflict, who started what, w</span>
  <span id="freeText1843466473387895508" style="display:none">I selected this book after reading a news story about a Palestinian lawyer personally financing its translation into Arabic for his friends and family to read, his name is Elias Khoury. He was moved by a personal account through the eyes of a young boy about the birth of Israel, its families and real world strife; he hoped for better understanding between the warring sides. I thought this must be some book. It is.<br />I've always been cloudy on the subject of the Israeli conflict, who started what, when and I wanted a good place to find it. This book didn't sway me to either side, but gave some depth to tension that still exists today. <br />Amos Oz's narrative moves haltingly forward then shifts to years earlier than back to where he left off, than jumps forward this time, than back again. Its easy to loose track of when the story is being told, how old the author is at any time and what clan he's talking about. He drops a lot of names, most of which should require a list of cast members and their purpose to the story, but after awhile it doesn't matter to the tone or the central characters of the book. <br />Mr. Oz surrounds you with light and darkness, rich smells, sounds and feelings. A sense of poetry pervades. A love of words and word play abounds. Everywhere he turns he is surrounded by lush, cherished books that seem to seep into his being. <br />He describes the method of his father on how he puts together a book, by accumulating research cards and pasting them together in sequence then he describes his own method, with scraps of paper filled with ideas spread out and taped together. That is how this book reads, a tapestry spread across a family bed, color, texture, and feelings. It reads as though he didn't know how he wanted to approach this patchwork of memories. Its how we all relate to our own memories, vignettes overlapping vignettes. It is very effective. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1843466473387895508" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8867888496941344912">I have been a fan of Amos Oz for years and years. My Michael and In the Land of Israel are two of my absolute favorite books, and now I understand why they both struck me so powerfully. Oz's life is the stuff of legend, and this book offers an intimate view of Israel's history: all the hopes and struggles of the pioneers, the pain of the diaspora generation, conveyed with honesty and affection.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_611631457" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/611631457" />
    <a title="Stephen Durrant" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1457001-stephen-durrant"><img alt="Stephen Durrant" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1362701086p2/1457001.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/611631457?book_show_action=true">May 09, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephen Durrant" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephen Durrant" href="/user/show/1457001-stephen-durrant">Stephen Durrant</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer611631457" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15267953576733910171">With some books one wants to write a summary or a review, with others one would prefer to sit down with a friend and just read out loud favorite passages. The latter are usually the books we like the best. They are so interesting, provocative, informative or amusing that we just want to share them. Amos Oz's "A Tale of Love and Darkness" is, for me, one such book. Oz is often mentioned, alongside Haruki Murakami, Philip Roth, and Javier Marias as a Nobel Prize favorite. Somehow I do not think he</span>
  <span id="freeText15267953576733910171" style="display:none">With some books one wants to write a summary or a review, with others one would prefer to sit down with a friend and just read out loud favorite passages. The latter are usually the books we like the best. They are so interesting, provocative, informative or amusing that we just want to share them. Amos Oz's "A Tale of Love and Darkness" is, for me, one such book. Oz is often mentioned, alongside Haruki Murakami, Philip Roth, and Javier Marias as a Nobel Prize favorite. Somehow I do not think he, nor perhaps any of these four, will ever emerge the victor. But he would be, in my opinion, a worthy recipient, and this book would probably make his strongest claim on the prize. "A Tale of Love and Darkness" is both a family story, sad as most such stories tend to be, and a fascinating description of the end of the British Mandate, the foundation of Israel, and the Arab-Israeli War of 1948. Amos Oz has a somewhat complicated political history, which gives richness to his view of these events. He was raised in a strongly Zionist family who had come to Israel from Poland (and had never stopped being "Europeans") and supported the fiery rhetoric of Menachim Begin and his Herut (the book contains a hilarious description of Oz as a little boy going to hear the "heroic" Begin). Later, Oz rebelled against the Israeli right and as a teenager became a Kibbutzim and began to express real concern about displaced Palestinians. The political and cultural descriptions and anecdotes in this book are unforgettable. To get some taste of this, one need only read, for example, his comments about the culture of writing in Jerusalem (296-299, beginning with the sentence "All Jerusalem in my childhood during the final years of British rule, sat at home and wrote") or his description of a brief meeting as a young soldier with the mythic Ben-Gurion (440-449, "He was an honest, cruel man; like most visionaries he did not stop to count the cost. Or perhaps he did stop for a moment and decided: let it cost whatever it costs," and so forth). But the real tale here, the one of "Love and Darkness," is a family tragedy. Oz's father is a Jewish intellectual who masters ten or eleven languages and is really only comfortable weaving endless etymologies through his many languages, buffering himself from real emotion with a thick texture of words. His mother was a dreamy story-teller, probably smarter than her husband, who slowly declined into isolation and finally suicide when her son Amos was only twelve years old. Like most Israeli women of her time, as Oz explains, she was expected to remain silent, however intelligent, when talk turned to the serious "male" subjects of literature, philosophy, and politics. These parents are both romantics, but romantics of a different breed as Oz explains in this final passage I just must share: "Both of my parents came to Jerusalem straight from the nineteenth century. My father had grown up on a concentrated diet of operatic, nationalistic, battle-thirsty romanticism (the Springtime of Nations, Sturm und Drang), whose marzipan peaks were sprinkle, like a splash of champagne, with the virile frenzy of Nietzsche. My mother, on the other hand, lived by the other Romantic canon, the introspective, melancholy menu of loneliness in a minor key, soaked in the suffering of broken-hearted, soulfull outcasts, infused with vague autumnal scents of fin de siècle decadence" (p 250).</span>
  <a data-text-id="15267953576733910171" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_345597908" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/345597908" />
    <a title="Maggi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/867546-maggi"><img alt="Maggi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1203895622p2/867546.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/345597908?book_show_action=true">Jun 09, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maggi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maggi" href="/user/show/867546-maggi">Maggi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer345597908" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7063073746984843310"> This sometimes long and sometimes tedious, yet beautiful and sometimes magical book took me forever to read, but it was worth the effort, a fascinating study of what it was to be a first generation native Israeli with Eastern European parents who narrowly escaped death in the 1930's by moving to the new Jewish nation. Just as in America, immigrant parents could be a trial: <i>Nobody in our neighborhood ever died from consumption or unrequited love or idealism. They were anything but reckless. </i> Oz'</span>
  <span id="freeText7063073746984843310" style="display:none"> This sometimes long and sometimes tedious, yet beautiful and sometimes magical book took me forever to read, but it was worth the effort, a fascinating study of what it was to be a first generation native Israeli with Eastern European parents who narrowly escaped death in the 1930's by moving to the new Jewish nation. Just as in America, immigrant parents could be a trial: <i>Nobody in our neighborhood ever died from consumption or unrequited love or idealism. They were anything but reckless. </i> Oz's love for his relatives is juxtaposed against his impatience with them brilliantly, sometimes humorously. <i>...My great-uncle Joseph, full of love and self-pity, vulnerable and craving recognition, brimming with childlike merriment, a happy man who always pretended to be miserable</i> or <i>My grandmother Schlomit arrived in Jerusalem straight form Vilna one hot summer's day in 1933, took one startled look at the sweaty markets, the colorful stalls, the bleating of goats, the swarming streets full of the cries of the hawkers .. and blood dripping from the necks of slaughtered chickens, she saw the shoulders and arms of Middle eastern men and the strident colors of the fruit and vegetables.. and immediately pronounced her final verdict: 'The Levant is full of germs.'</i> <br /><br />Oz depicts the the whole crazy process of a child who arises out of the rubble of persecution into a new world where his parents don't quite fit: <i>Even people like my parents who knew Hebrew well were not entirely its masters.... perhaps that is how a shortsighted driver feels, trying to find his way at night through a warren of side streets in a strange city in an unfamiliar car... When we were conquered by the Poles, by the Red Army, and then by the Nazis, when they simply took us all and shot us in ditches and covered us with earth. Then there was Stalin again.. Rovno being thrown hand to hand like a puppy being teased by Russia-Poland-Russia-Germany-Russia. </i> The legacy of sorrow and pain is tragically embodied by Oz's mother's depression and suicide, and the way that event informed his entire life, unspoken about by his father, whom Oz admired and loved and yet scorned and hurt too. This father-son relationship is explored in its human complexity and familiarity. When his father comes to visit him in the kibbutz where Amos has gone to live (and where he stayed for the next 30 years) in an act of rebellion against the family ideals, he is unforgiving of his youthful zeal: <i>I dragged him off for a comprehensive tour of the length and breadth of Hulda. I did not bother to ask him if he would rather rest. I did not bother to offer him a cold shower, or show him the toilets. Like a sergeant-major on a base for new recruits I rushed my poor father along, red-faced, panting, mopping his face all the time.. and all the time I lectured him about the principles of the kibbutz, agricultural economy, the advantages of socialism, the contribution of the kibbutz to Israel's military victories... I did not let him utter a word.. I talked and I talked and I talked.</i> Later when they say goodbye, <i>He looked so awkward and sad that I almost touched his shoulder. But I didn't. For the first time in my life, my father seemed afraid of me. I would have liked to help him.. but I was as inhibited as he was.</i><br /><br />The formation of Israel, the violence the immigrant Jews faced even after escaping violence in their home countries, is shown in all its complexity as well. <i>Maybe the Arabs were being stirred up to hate us.. but surely we'd be able to explain to them and persuade them that our return to the Land represented only a blessing for them, economically, medically, culturally, in every way. We thought that soon, in a few years, the Jews would be the majority here, and as soon as that happened, we'd show the whole world how to treat a minority-- our own minority, the Arabs. We, who had always been an oppressed minority, would treat our Arab minority fairly, justly, generously, we would share our homeland with them, share everything with them... it was a pretty dream.</i><br /><br />There is no way to detail the many, many pieces of writing that take one's breath away in this book: <i>The sun has set... though the firmament has not let go of the sun but has seized in its claws and managed to tear the train of its many-colored cloak and is now trying on its booty, using the cirrus clouds as a dressmaker's dummy putting on light like a garment.. shivering like the broken lines sketched underwater by a fast moving school of fish. </i> Yet the long lists of authors and references to academic works are too frequent. The point is well made, and frequently, that the Jewish tradition of intellectualism (as evidenced in Oz's own close family members) is long and proud, but sometimes uselessly anachronistic as well. <br /><br />This is a book of rare honesty and reflection, humor and joy, and profound humanity. Taking the time to wade through the more arcane historical information is a lesson in humility and education, outweighed by the sensitivity and perspective offered by this brilliant writer.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7063073746984843310" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/54642985" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/54642985?book_show_action=true">May 01, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicole" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicole" href="/user/show/201360-nicole">Nicole</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer54642985" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14829701371318455767">This book snuck up on me and made me fall in love. I wasn't sure how I felt about it for the first hundred pages or so. I thought the writing was exquisite, but I wasn't sure how interested I was by a story about an only child growing up in his dreamy, book-filled world. But as I kept reading, I found myself pulled further and further into his world. So many poignant details, so many touching family stories, so many tragic human interactions.<br/><br/>Some of my favorite parts:<br/>- The author's mother's stor</span>
  <span id="freeText14829701371318455767" style="display:none">This book snuck up on me and made me fall in love. I wasn't sure how I felt about it for the first hundred pages or so. I thought the writing was exquisite, but I wasn't sure how interested I was by a story about an only child growing up in his dreamy, book-filled world. But as I kept reading, I found myself pulled further and further into his world. So many poignant details, so many touching family stories, so many tragic human interactions.<br /><br />Some of my favorite parts:<br />- The author's mother's stories. All complex, intricate, and gorgeous, yet sordid.<br />- The bird singing Fur Elise. I loved the way it was threaded through the book and placed at the very end.<br />- Seeing a child's idealistic perspective on the creation of the Israeli state and the hopes of making peace with the Palestinians, and the way that changed as the violence escalated and he developed a more jaded adult view.<br /><br />Also, kudos to the translator, Nicholas de Lange. I had a hard time believing this was a translation. Wow. The language was just so beautiful, without a trace of awkwardness. There were a lot of parts that must have been tricky to do (the father's jokes and puns, the rhyming, the extremely specific, detailed descriptions, etc.). I am in awe of his work. Of course, he had an amazing original to work from, but still.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14829701371318455767" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Mary Stergioti" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/46486105-mary-stergioti"><img alt="Mary Stergioti" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1496692188p2/46486105.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mary Stergioti" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mary Stergioti" href="/user/show/46486105-mary-stergioti">Mary Stergioti</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789600338171" href="/book/show/22882204">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1378810266" class="readable"

<div id="review_304333597" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/304333597" />
    <a title="Mom" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6503374-mom"><img alt="Mom" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/304333597?book_show_action=true">Mar 31, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mom" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mom" href="/user/show/6503374-mom">Mom</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer304333597" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7036258127533987590">Well.... This is a difficult book for me to rate. A beautifully written book, tragic, historic, with long brilliantly descriptive passages, a memoir of amazing times and a boy of incredible talent and sensitivity. Still, often the book was tedious, over-written, repetitive. It took me weeks to finish the book, when most books I devour in a day or two.<br/><br/>A Tale of Love and Darkness is the memoir of a young boy growing up in Jerusalem when it was still under the control of the British and considered </span>
  <span id="freeText7036258127533987590" style="display:none">Well.... This is a difficult book for me to rate. A beautifully written book, tragic, historic, with long brilliantly descriptive passages, a memoir of amazing times and a boy of incredible talent and sensitivity. Still, often the book was tedious, over-written, repetitive. It took me weeks to finish the book, when most books I devour in a day or two.<br /><br />A Tale of Love and Darkness is the memoir of a young boy growing up in Jerusalem when it was still under the control of the British and considered part of Palestine. He tells of his grandparents in Russia and Poland, and of the other members of his scholarly, literary family. Interspersed with the tale of his family is the story of the founding of Israel, of the UN mandate, and the wars with the Arab states. The story jumps back and forth chronologically, from his grandparents, to his own childhood, to his adult years in a kibbutz, and back to his grandparents. His parents are ill-matched and there is more than a bit of tragedy in their relationship, although certainly Amos is surrounded by love and devotion. From near the beginning, we know that his mother is going to commit suicide (when he is just 12), but it is not until the last few pages that the full story is told. <br /><br />The writing is brilliant, the descriptions of people captivating, and the stories, both of his personal history and of the history of Israel, are riveting.<br /><br />So what didn't I like about the book? Well, it is repetitive (OZ must repeat twenty times that his father disliked silence and had to fill it with chatter and witticisms -- something he could have said once, then let us notice...). Oz also over-used lists. The lists were poetically descriptive, hypnotic, even ("...the other Jerusalem: a city of old cypress trees that were more black than green, streets of stone walls, interlaced grilles, cornices, and dark walls, the alien, silent, aloof, shrouded Jerusalem, the Abyssinian, Muslim, pilgrim, Ottoman city, the strange, missionary city of crusaders and Templars, the Greek, Armenian, Italian, brooding, Anglican, Greek Orthodox city, the monastic, Coptic, Catholic, Lutheran, Scottish, Sunni, Shiite, Sufi, Alawite city, swept by the sound of bells and the wail of the muezzin, thick with pine trees, frightening yet alluring, with concealed enchantments, warrens of narrow streets...." etc!! ) but sometimes they were just too much. I found myself scanning ahead to get back to the story. The descriptions were the same -- long and beautiful, but over-used and over-long.<br /><br />In the end, I loved the book because of the brilliant writing and the sad, tender story of his childhood. I came to know intimately, and care for, his parents. I appreciated reading about the founding of Israel through a non-historian's eyes. I recommend the book but you'll need time and a willingness to persevere through sometimes tedious passages. <br /><br /> <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7036258127533987590" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer578574084" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16485066631142211986">The shelves I have chosen for this book say it well. This beautifully written,thoughtful book is more than the autobiography of a person;it is a deep history of a place, and how that place came to be the city we again know as Jerusalem. In addition are the backstories, of life as it became unlivable for the Jews of mostly Eastern Europe in the generations leading up to 'the final solution' for their troublesome fate.<br/><br/>Oz is a beautiful,lyrical writer. He brings it all alive with an immediacey ofte</span>
  <span id="freeText16485066631142211986" style="display:none">The shelves I have chosen for this book say it well. This beautifully written,thoughtful book is more than the autobiography of a person;it is a deep history of a place, and how that place came to be the city we again know as Jerusalem. In addition are the backstories, of life as it became unlivable for the Jews of mostly Eastern Europe in the generations leading up to 'the final solution' for their troublesome fate.<br /><br />Oz is a beautiful,lyrical writer. He brings it all alive with an immediacey often lacking in the genre. Moreover, though I often lamented the fact that I chose to carry this hardcover edition on my walking tour from the Dead Sea to Jerusalem,without the liesure to read more than a dozen pages, when I was able to get back to it,the timing couldn't have been more appropriate. While outside my window, all Jerusalem was celebrating Independence Day, I was caught up in the firsthand account of what it was like to live through that war. Although I am still a pacifist,I now understand how it was an inevitable response to the situation facing the new state,immediately after it was established. <br /><br />The book is nevertheless in no way chauvinistic or lopsided. It is a careful look at the situation as perceived by a sensitive soul. The tragedy of his family saga is told with clarity,not pathos. In the end,of course I cried, and wanted nothing more than to find Amos Oz and give him a hug. He has my endless gratitude for opening my eyes to a reality I have always glossed over.<br /><br />...in the last resort maybe all we are left with is the freedom to laugh at our condition or to lament it,to play the game or throw in our hand,to try more or less to understand what is and what isn't the case,or to give up and try not to understand-in a nutshell,the choice is between going through this life awake or in a kind of stupor. p166</span>
  <a data-text-id="16485066631142211986" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_965922754" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/965922754" />
    <a title="Susan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1652278-susan"><img alt="Susan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1224862599p2/1652278.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/965922754?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Susan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Susan" href="/user/show/1652278-susan">Susan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer965922754" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11387757502353384056">Truely a book which straddles the fence between a novel and a memoir,and succeeds brilliantly in y opinion. A memoir doesn't always have a central idea of theme and this one really does: his mother's suicide and coming to terms with it, a process in which I suspect this book played an important part. We learn relatively early that his mother committed suicide when he was 12 and that as soon as he could get away, he left his father and joined to Kibbutz to be another kind a Jew in Israel, not a b</span>
  <span id="freeText11387757502353384056" style="display:none">Truely a book which straddles the fence between a novel and a memoir,and succeeds brilliantly in y opinion. A memoir doesn't always have a central idea of theme and this one really does: his mother's suicide and coming to terms with it, a process in which I suspect this book played an important part. We learn relatively early that his mother committed suicide when he was 12 and that as soon as he could get away, he left his father and joined to Kibbutz to be another kind a Jew in Israel, not a booking, intellectual ultra observation with roots in Eastern Europe but an energetic, liberal agricultural worker,the kind who, it seemed to him was really going to build the new country of Israel. After all his was born in Israel, lived as a child through the war for independence and played on the borders in Jerusalem as a growing boy. He considered Hebrew his native language. He doesn't say whether he learned Russian but it seems his parents could talk in that language when they didn't want him to understand.<br /><br />One of the most interesting parts of this European backstory was his mother's school days in a Hebrew school in Rovno (which was first Poland then then Russia (Ukraine). (The government evidently encourage the Hebrew schools, hoping that the Jews would immigrate.) this part is told from the point of view of, her younger sister Sonia who almost becomes the narrator in this part and brings the reader much closer to this part of Oz's heritage than any other. I found it brilliantly managed.<br /><br />The early part of the book lays out that Eastern European background which was his heritag, the writers, the intellectuals, the books, the ideas as well as the places and the people. Both sides of the family were Zionists who watched and waited and then immigrated to Israel in the 30ies. One brother of his father chose to stay in Vilnius because he had a good university position and hoped for advancement and he and his family disappeared in the Holocaust. They were all relatively poor in Israel and like so many immigrants stuck to their own kind in the new country. (I found the descriptions of Jerusalem and to some extent Tel Aviv where his mother's sisters lived, interesting too since I've never been to Israel.)<br /><br />Gradually the mother is mentioned more and more but the reader never really understands what happened to her until the very end which is carefully plotted and very emotional—brilliantly sustained suspense and emotion, much more like a novel than a memoir.<br /><br />I've never read any of Oz's fiction but I probably will now. By the way, in rebelling against his family, he changed his name from Klausner to Oz because it meant powerful, I presume NOT like the Wizard of Oz.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11387757502353384056" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('91s8WV1wfBS4dY8ZLjFpbzmc6k2OLOh7YnqLCAXgn4blxf5Do/qgjToxR1/j4dCVru1Fy9jCjYPRC6GxCiFm0A==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_207333'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/207333.A_Pigeon_and_a_Boy?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">A Pigeon and a Boy<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/104544.Meir_Shalev?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Meir Shalev<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.04 avg rating &mdash; 2,920 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2006\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer301562363651242868\">From the internationally acclaimed Israeli writer Meir Shalev comes a mesmerizing novel of two love stories, separated by half a century but connected by one enchanting act of devotion. During the 1948 War of Independence--a time when pigeons are sti<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText301562363651242868\" style=\"display:none\">From the internationally acclaimed Israeli writer Meir Shalev comes a mesmerizing novel of two love stories, separated by half a century but connected by one enchanting act of devotion. During the 1948 War of Independence--a time when pigeons are still used to deliver battlefield messages--a gifted young pigeon handler is mortally wounded. In the moments before his death, he dispatches one last pigeon. The bird is carrying his extraordinary gift to the girl he has loved since adolescence. Intertwined with this story is the contemporary tale of Yair Mendelsohn, who has his own legacy from the 1948 war. Yair is a tour guide specializing in bird-watching trips who, in middle age, falls in love again with a childhood girlfriend. His growing passion for her, along with a gift from his mother on her deathbed, becomes the key to a life he thought no longer possible. Unforgettable in both its particulars and its sweep, A Pigeon and A Boy is a tale of lovers then and now--of how deeply we love, of what home is, and why we, like pigeons trained to fly in one direction only, must eventually return to it. In a voice that is at once playful, wise, and altogether beguiling, Meir Shalev tells a story as universal as war and as intimate as a winged declaration of love. In a fit of preemptive grief and magical thinking, she sets out for a hike in the Galilee, leaving no forwarding information for the “notifiers” who might darken her door with the worst possible news. Recently estranged from her husband, Ilan, she drags along an unlikely companion: their former best friend and her former lover Avram, once a brilliant artistic spirit. Avram served in the army alongside Ilan when they were young, but their lives were forever changed one weekend when the two jokingly had Ora draw lots to see which of them would get the few days’ leave being offered by their commander—a chance act that sent Avram into Egpyt and the Yom Kippur War, where he was brutally tortured as POW. \n\nIn the aftermath, a virtual hermit, he refused to keep in touch with the family and has never met the boy. Now, as Ora and Avram sleep out in the hills, ford rivers, and cross valleys, avoiding all news from the front, she gives him the gift of Ofer, word by word; she supplies the whole story of her motherhood, a retelling that keeps Ofer very much alive for Ora and for the reader, and opens Avram to human bonds undreamed of in his broken world. Their walk has a “war and peace” rhythm, as their conversation places the most hideous trials of war next to the joys and anguish of raising children. Never have we seen so clearly the reality and surreality of daily life in Israel, the currents of ambivalence about war within one household, and the burdens that fall on each generation anew. \n\nGrossman’s rich imagining of a family in love and crisis makes for one of the great antiwar novels of our time.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"15505988161344481052\" href=\"#\" Here we meet Shalev’s amazing Grandma Tonia, who <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText17558048722364121444\" style=\"display:none\">From the author of the acclaimed novel A Pigeon and a Boy comes a charming tale of family ties, over-the-top housekeeping, and the sport of storytelling in Nahalal, the village of Meir Shalev’s birth. Here we meet Shalev’s amazing Grandma Tonia, who arrived in Palestine by boat from Russia in 1923 and lived in a constant state of battle with what she viewed as the family’s biggest enemy in their new land: dirt. Grandma Tonia was never seen without a cleaning rag over her shoulder. She received visitors outdoors. She allowed only the most privileged guests to enter her spotless house. Hilarious and touching, Grandma Tonia and her regulations come richly to life in a narrative that circles around the arrival into the family’s dusty agricultural midst of the big, shiny American sweeper sent as a gift by Great-uncle Yeshayahu (he who had shockingly emigrated to the sinful capitalist heaven of Los Angeles!). America, to little Meir and to his forebears, was a land of hedonism and enchanting progress; of tempting luxuries, dangerous music, and degenerate gum-chewing; and of women with painted fingernails. The sweeper, a stealth weapon from Grandpa Aharon’s American brother meant to beguile the hardworking socialist household with a bit of American ease, was symbolic of the conflicts and visions of the family in every respect. The fate of Tonia’s “svieeperrr”—hidden away for decades in a spotless closed-off bathroom after its initial use—is a family mystery that Shalev determines to solve. The result, in this cheerful translation by Evan Fallenberg, is pure delight, as Shalev brings to life the obsessive but loving Tonia, the pioneers who gave his childhood its spirit of wonder, and the grit and humor of people building ever-new lives.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"17558048722364121444\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n An extraordinary bond of love and mutual respect unites the sisters -- polar opposites from their appearances to their tempers. Huda, the narrator of the story, is thin and withdrawn and, after abandoning her chance at marriage a few years back, has prematurely resigned herself to the monotonous life of an old maid. Her younger sister, Mary, is voluptuous, carnal, and perennially unemployed. Wrapped in the love of their sometimes bitter mother, their iconoclast grandfather, and the cheerful and omnipresent neighbor Jamilla, the sisters\' lives change when a peculiar young Russian Jewish immigrant, Alex, moves into the upstairs flat. The melodies of the soulful trumpet player become the intoxicating theme music for Huda\'s unexpected reawakening -- and for Mary\'s dangerous foray into a love triangle with the heir of the local Muslim mob and her country cousin.\n\nMichael\'s internationally acclaimed novel is a major achievement, illuminating the vast range of interlocking relationships between Jews and Arabs, Muslims and Not far from the apartment lives a family grieving for their eldest son who was killed in Lebanon. The younger brother left behind, Yotam, forgotten by his parents, turns to Amir for support. He knows that this house is the one from which his family was driven by the Jews when he was a boy, and to which his mother still has a rusty key. Patriarch Abraham Levy, the proud descendant of fifteen generations of Sephardic Jews, and his wife, the convert Sarah, a monumental, generous woman, illiterate and complex, establish themselves in a village to the west of Jerusalem and become the center of a sprawling, colorful family whose passions, suffering, and unexpected fates partake of both the real and the mythic, not to mention the miraculous. Esau\'s eponymous narrator is one of a pair of near-sighted Levy twins, who have only a single set of eyeglasses between them. The life choice each boy makes as a result of this childhood experience determines the course of the novel: Jacob, who wears the eyeglasses most of the time, follows his father and becomes the village baker, marrying Leah, fathering children, and shouldering the responsibility of ancestral tradition; while the narrator, with his willfully blurred vision, is cursed and disinherited by his beloved mother and leaves his family and village to become a writer in the United States. After thirty years of exile, he returns home and offers us the brilliant and deeply moving mosaic that is the story of the Levy family. Like his progenitor, Meir Shalev, the narrator finds his portion of the world in books, and the two of them share a Rabelaisian appetite for story and character, an exuberant playfulness, a mischievous sense of irony, and a fascination with the sensuality of childhood, those images, sounds, and odors at the origin of all memory and roots.<\/span>\n  <a בדרום אמריקה נפגש עם מכשפים אינדיאניים, טיפס על פסגות מושלגות ודלילות חמצן, וביקר בעיירות יהודיות נשכחות שחלקן אפילו לא מופיע על המפה. \n\nעל הספר:\nמני פלג, אלמן טרי בן שישים, סוגר להפתעת ילדיו את העסק ויוצא לטיול תרמילאים בדרום אמריקה. כעבור כמה חודשים בדרכים הוא נעלם בנסיבות לא ברורות, ובנו דורי נאלץ לצאת למסע חיפושים בעקבותיו. בקצה אחר של הגלובוס - ענבר, מפיקת רדיו צעירה, שוהה בביקור קצר אצל עושים גיבורי נוילנד את דרכם אל נקודת הרתיחה של הרומן, אל מקום מסקרן ומלהיב בהיסטוריה החלופית של ישראל, שממנו יחזרו שונים. אשכול נבו שב ומפליא בבריאת דמויות המדברות אל לב הקורא כמו חברים ותיקים. הפעם הוא צולל אל תהומות הרגש של שתי משפחות ומאיר שם את מעמקיהן ואת היחסים המורכבים של ישראלים ויהודים עם אופציית הנדודים. נוילנד הוא רומן סוחף ונועז שכל גיבוריו נמצאים בתנועה, ולכולם המסע מזמן אפשרות נדירה ביותר ברשת מאז הקמתה. \n\nארבעה בתים וגעגוע ו-משאלה אחת ימינה תורגמו לשבע שפות. ארבעה בתים וגעגוע זכה בפרס ע&quot;ש ריימונד וואלייר בצרפת. הצגה על בסיס ארבעה בתים וגעגוע הועלתה באפריל השנה על ידי According to the government it doesn’t exist; according to the military it must be defended. On this contested land, Othniel Assis—under the wary gaze of the neighboring Palestinian village—plants asparagus, arugula, and cherry tomatoes, and he installs goats—and his ever-expanding family. As Othniel cheerfully manipulates government agencies, more settlers arrive, and, amid a hodge-podge of shipping containers and mobile homes, the outpost takes root. One of the settlement’s steadfast residents is Gabi Kupper, a one-time free spirit and kibbutz-dweller, who undergoes a religious awakening. The delicate routines of Gabi’s new life are thrown into turmoil with the sudden arrival of Roni, his prodigal brother, who, years after venturing to America in search of fortune, arrives at Gabi’s door, penniless. To the settlement’s dismay, Roni soon hatches a plan to sell the “artisanal” olive oil from the Palestinian village to Tel Aviv yuppies. When a curious Washington Post correspondent stumbles into their midst, Ma’aleh Hermesh C becomes the focus of an international diplomatic scandal and faces its greatest test yet. By turns serious and satirical, The Hilltop brilliantly skewers the complex, often absurd reality of life in Israel, the West Bank settlers, and the nation\'s relationship to the United States, and makes a startling parallel between today’s settlements and the kibbutz movement of Gabi and Roni’s youth. Rich with humor and insight, Assaf Gavron’s novel is the first fiction to grapple with one of the most charged geo-political issues of our time, and he has written a masterpiece.<\/span>\n  <a <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer2107163138294658701">Amos Oz (Hebrew: עמוס עוז‎; born Amos Klausner) was an Israeli writer, novelist, journalist and intellectual. He was also a professor of literature at Ben-Gurion University in Beersheba. He was regarded as Israel's most famous living author.<br/><br/>Oz's work has been published in 42 languages in 43 countries, and has received many honours and awards, among them the Legion of Honour of France, the Goethe P</span>
  <span id="freeText2107163138294658701" style="display:none">Amos Oz (Hebrew: עמוס עוז‎; born Amos Klausner) was an Israeli writer, novelist, journalist and intellectual. He was also a professor of literature at Ben-Gurion University in Beersheba. He was regarded as Israel's most famous living author.<br /><br />Oz's work has been published in 42 languages in 43 countries, and has received many honours and awards, among them the Legion of Honour of France, the Goethe Prize, the Prince of Asturias Award in Literature, the Heinrich Heine Prize and the Israel Prize. In 2007, a selection from the Chinese translation of A Tale of Love and Darkness was the first work of modern Hebrew literature to appear in an official Chinese textbook.<br /><br />Since 1967, Oz had been a prominent advocate of a two-state solution to the Israeli–Palestinian conflict.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2107163138294658701" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

            <span class="readable">&ldquo;There are lots of women who are attracted to tyrannical men. Like moths to a flame. And there are some women who do not need a hero or even a stormy lover but a friend. Just remember that when you grow up. Steer clear of the tryant lovers, and try to locate the ones who are looking for a man as a friend, not because they are feeling empty themselves but because they enjoy making you full too. And remember that friendship between a woman and a man is something much more precious and rare than love: love is actually something quite gross and even clumsy compared to friendship. Friendship includes a measure of sensitivity, attentiveness, generosity, and a finely tuned sense of moderation.&rdquo;</span>
